He had the gift of reading their secret sins, and won them with such gentleness that he was called ' the loadstone of hearts.' Consulted by the duke on all matters, civil as well as spiritual, he was enabled to succour the oppressed of every class, and to distribute immense sums in charity; 66,000l. were calculated to have thus passed through his hands. In the famine of 1678 he fed whole multitudes daily from the royal stores he had collected. The duke earnestly pressed on him the archbishopric of Turin; but Sebastian refused this and every other mark of honour, and sought only the contempt of men. Thus, when hissister visited him, he borrowed a state coach from one or his noble penitents, and made her enter the city in it, clad in her peasant's dress, that all men might know his lowly birth. The dying, however, were his special care; for, he said, all other assistance to our neighbour is useless, if we neglect him at the last. He went at once to every sick call, and often started unsummoned, being miraculously awakened from sleep, to seek out sick and dying persons in need of a priest. BB

### Zeal for Souls.

Sebastian's zeal sprung from the conviction that his health, time, and talents were but lent to him to be employed in the service of God and of his neighbour. Do we feel that we are each responsible for these gifts in our own degree?

' He who loves God never says, " It is enough:" but the more he has laboured the more he is ready to labour.'-B. Sebastian.

Sebastian, persuaded that obedience alone could insure God's blessing on his labours, would never undertake any work without the leave of his superriors; and they, knowing his sanctity, designedly mortified him in the matter. He applied once, when quite an old man, for leave to make his weekly visit to the prison; the Superior refused him abruptly, and reproved him before a stranger for love of dissipation. Another time he had actually started on a pious visit to Rome, when he was suddenly recalled without any reason being given. On each occasion he obeyed without a murmur, though he had himself been many years Superior, and was the senior father of the Congregation. Thus true zeal is never self-willed.

' I must work the works of Him who sent me whilst it is day; the night cometh when no man can work.'-John ix. 4.

## January 30 — B. Sebastian Valfrè.

**Virtue:** Perseverance.

### Life

In December 1709 Sebastian took leave of a friend with the words: ' Till we meet again in Paradise.' He was then in strong health; but, like S. Philip, had been warned of his coming end. His last day's ministry tells his life. On Jan. 24, 1710, he preached at a convent, visited and consoled a condemned criminal who was to die on the morrow, and called at the office of the Inquisition, of which he was a member. It was Friday, and, observant to the last, Sebastian hastened home to take the discipline. The oratory was damp, and he caught a fatal cold. In spite of increasing pains he said Mass the next morning, went afterwards on the arm of a brother to speak to a poor woman who sent for him to the door, but was then obliged to take to his bed. On the fourth day he received the last Sacraments in presence of all the Community, and having begged their pardon for his many faults, at their earnest request gave them his blessing. To the duke, who visited him, he gave as his last advice, ' Be ever dutiful to the Vicar of Christ.' He lingered on another day in great pain, but in constant prayer; being asked after one spasm where he suffered, he replied, ' Everywhere.' At length, on Jan. 30, after bowing his head to receive absolution once more, he tranquilly expired. The Saint was declared 'Venerable ' by Pius VIII., and ' Blessed ' by Gregory XVI. on May 20, 1831.

### Perseverance.

When Sebastian was asked if he regretted dying, he replied: ' As I have no attachment to anything in this world, why should I regret leaving it?" Detachment is the secret of perseverance.

' Crosses borne with patience are like storms at sea, which seem to swamp the ship, which they are really bearing towards the shore. Life without a cross is the heaviest cross of all.'B. Sebastian.

Sebastian confided to a priest, a penitent of his who was in trouble, that his own life was nothing but contradictions. He had the greatest aversion to dealings with great people or with women, yet was constantly thrown with both of them. His theological lectures, owing to the inattention of the students, seemed at times utterly fruitless. The office of Superior was most burdensome to him, and his dislike to preaching such that at times he was obliged to bind himself by a vow to go on with it. He had so strong an antipathy to a certain person, that he could not have lived in his society but by the same means. Yet he thanked God who had thus detached him from everything, and left him the love of his salvation alone.

' It is good for me that Thou hast humbled me. that I may learn Thy justifications.'-Ps. cxviii. 71.

## May 18 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** Angelic Purity.

### Life

Philip Neri was born in Florence on July 21st, 1515. As a child he was noted for a rare modesty, seriousness, delight in prayer and in the things of God. He was ever most obedient; and his father's step-mother, who brought him up, loved him as her own son, and on her deathbed repeated his name again and again, saying that it refreshed her even to think of him. For his pure, sweet, winning ways he was known among his companions as ' good Pippo.' His piety was simple and grave; he would pray, sing psalms, listen to sermons, rather than dress altars and the like. And his prayers seemed to be always answered. He once recovered a gold necklace which he had lost in the streets, by simply praying for it. His virtue was tried by many and grievous temptations. On one occasion, when he was addressed by evil persons, who wished to lead him into sin, he spoke to them with such fervour of the foulness of their intent that he brought them to repentance; and when some persons had been hidden in his room, and had locked the door to prevent his escape, he knelt in prayer until these wretched creatures were forced to make their escape in shame and confusion. His purity shone on his face, beamed brightly from his eyes, even to extreme old age, and breathed from his hands and from his whole person a fragrance so rare, so unearthly, that within its presence all evil thoughts died away of themselves.

### Angelic Purity.

S. Philip bore unstained to the grave the purity of his youth. The touch of his hand was enough to drive away temptations. One of his penitents was always freed from the tempter by saying, at Philip's bidding, ' I will accuse you to Philip.' Make him your refuge and advocate in time of trial.

' Never trust to yourself, either on the ground of experience, or length of time, or age, or sickness; but always fly from every occasion of danger as long as you have but strength to raise your eyelids.'-S. Philip.

S. Philip gave young men five rules for keeping themselves pure: to shun bad company; not to pamper their bodies; to avoid idleness; to be diligent in prayer; and to frequent the Sacraments, especially confession. He said that humility was the true and sure guardian of chastity; and that when we hear of any one having fallen, we should be moved to pity and not to disdain; for that a want of compassion is a forerunner of our own speedy fall. He kept the most exact guard over his senses, and was wont to say every day, ' Lord, be with me this day, lest I betray Thee.' 'Who shall ascend into the mountain of the Lord; or who shall stand in His holy place? The innocent in hands and clean of heart.'Ps. xxiii. 3, 4.

## May 19 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** God alone.

### Life

WHEN Philip was sixteen years of age his father sent him to San Germano, near Naples, to learn the business and inherit the fortune of a cousin of his. His future thus seemed marked out for him and secured. While he was diligent and exact in his duties, he spent his spare time, and often his nights, in prayer before the crucifix in a lonely chapel amidst the mountains. At the end of two years he could no longer resist the drawing he felt to a higher life, and in spite of his cousin's urgent and affectionate entreaties he resolved to turn from the wealth which would be his, and to take God alone for his portion. In the year 1533 he set out for Rome, alone, with no provision for the way, no resources, no plans, simply following the inward call of God. He was guided to the house of a Florentine gentleman whose two sons he educated, training them in such virtuous ways that they grew up as angels of purity and innocence. His life was one of extreme austerity. His single daily meal was of bread and water, with sometimes herbs or a few olives, and he would pass three days without food. His few clothes hung on a cord stretched across his room, the floor of which was his bed, and thus he lived, according to his heart's desire, for God alone, in absolute detachment and poverty, with no thought or care for the future, his life hidden with Christ inGod.

### God alone.

It was a saying of Philip's, 'All the love we bear to creatures is so much taken from God.' This great thought made him turn away from the fair worldly prospect before him; and he promised a brother of the congregation to take him to Paradise, if only he would close his heart to all desire of wealth.

'Give me but ten men wholly detached, and with them I will convert the world.'-S. Philip.

One day Philip called to him Francesco Zazzara, a young man who was studying law with a view to advancement at court. The youth knelt at his feet, and Philip, gently caressing him as he spoke, set out before him, one by one, the daydreams of his youthful ambition. ' Happy you!' he said; ' you are studying now; then one day you will be doctor, and will gain money, and raise your family; then one day you may even be a prelate, till you have nothing more to long for.' It all seemed natural to Francesco; these were the very dreams of his fancy and his hope. And then Philip drew the youth's head tenderly to his heart, and whispered in his ear, ' And then l' Francesco went home, with the echo of these words in his heart, 'I am studying now;. and then I' He gave up all for God, and served Him in the Congregation until death.

'And he went out, not knowing whither he went.'-Hebrews xi. 8.

## May 20 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** The Spirit of Prayer.

### Life

WHEN Philip thought he had studied enough for his soul's good, he sold his books, gave their price to the poor, and applied his whole soul to prayer and the attainment of perfection. He increased his mortifications, slept but little, and mostly on the bare ground, disciplined himself with small iron chains, and lived a life of silence, calm amidst the tumult of the great city, alone amidst its busy multitudes. At night he would go round the seven churches, a distance of about twelve miles, and if they were closed he would remain in prayer at their doors. He would pray at times for forty hours without intermission; and so overpowering were the graces and consolations vouchsafed to him, that he would roll on the ground, crying, ' No more, Lord, no more!" At Whitsuntide, 1544, Philip being then in his thirtieth year, while he was praying most fervently in the catacomb of S. Sebastian for the gift of the Holy Ghost, there came suddenly from heaven as it were a ball of fire, which entered into his breast, bursting asunder two of his ribs, and leaving a swelling as large as the fist. This swelling continued throughout his life for fifty years, giving him no pain, only an intense glow of heat. It would palpitate joyously and impetuously when he was speaking of the things of God, or praying, saying Mass, giving absolution and Holy Com munion, and it filled his whole soul with the love ofGod.

### The Spirit of Prayer.

S. Philip was much opposed to irregularity in spiritual exercises, and he especially warned his sons against loading themselves with a great variety of devotions, advising them to be content with a few, and to keep to those few most faithfully.

'A man without prayer is a dumb beast.'-S. Philip.

S. Philip taught that the true preparation for prayer is constant mortification; for that an immortified soul can no more pray than an unfledged bird can fly. To one who asked him how to pray he said, ' Be humble and obedient, and the Holy Ghost will teach you.' As helps to prayer he advised spiritual reading and the Lives of the Saints; and he said we should not read out of curiosity or in a hurry, but leisurely and a little at a time, pausing when we feel ourselves touched with compunction or moved to devotion. And he added that we should carefully obey and follow the inspiration which God gives us in prayer. Under his direction persons of every class attained to great perfection in prayer, and received extraordinary favours from God.

' The Spirit helpeth our infirmity; for we know not what we should pray for as we ought, but the Spirit Himself asketh for us.'-Romans viii. 26.

## May 21 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** Daily Mass.

### Life

AFTER five years of solitude and prayer, Philip felt constrained to labour for souls. So he began to go about the squares and shops, saying to the young men, ' Well, my brothers, when shall we begin to do good? In this way he won very many to God, not only of the careless and tepid, but of the most hardened sinners. A few of these met together with Philip to receive the Sacraments, and for other pious exercises; and on the first Sunday in each month they watched in turn before the Blessed Sacrament. Thus was introduced into Rome the Forty Hours' prayer, which had begun six years before at Milan. In 1550 they set about receiving poor convalescents and the numerous pilgrims who flocked to Rome especially during the Jubilee. They began with a small house, which in time expanded into the great establishment of the SS. Trinità dei Pellegrini. And now his confessor, seeing his great power with souls, urged him to prepare for holy orders; and, in spite of his humble reluctance, he was ordained priest on May 23d, 1551. He then went to live with his confessor, and a few other holy priests, at the church of S. Girolamo della Carità. Here Philip began the practice which he never interrupted of saying Mass daily; and he said it was a great mistake to omit Mass for the sake of rest or recreation; for that he who looks for recreation out of the Creator, or for rest out of Christ, would never find either.

### Daily Mass.

The true preparation of a good priest, said S. Philip, is so to live as to be ready at any moment to say Mass or receive Communion; and he said the same thing of laymen, according to their state.

'To begin well and end better, two things are necessary: to be devout to the most holy Mother of God, and to hear Mass daily, where there is no lawful hindrance.'-S. Philip.

S. Philip's devotion to Holy Mass was such that he was often rapt into ecstasy at he thought of celebrating. At times during his Mass the predella and the whole altar would vibrate with the ardent beating of his heart. His hand trembled with joy as he touched the chalice. He was often so absorbed in God that he needed to be roused to continue his Mass. At the Elevation he could scarcely lower his extended arms, and his body would be seen raised from earth. To conceal these favours, Philip got leave to say Mass in a small private chapel. There, after the Agnus Dei, the server would lock the door and leave the Saint for two hours alone. What there passed between his soul and God who can tell? His server said that he looked as one who had just breathed out his soul to God.

' His sacrifices were consumed with fire every day.'-Ecclus. xlv. 17.

## May 22 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** Love of Souls.

### Life

S. PHILIP lived in troubled times, when amidst wars and civil strife men had forgotten God. His first aim therefore was to bring them back to the Holy Sacraments. To this end he was always ready to receive those who came to him. Before sunrise he had heard many confessions in his own room; at daybreak he would go down into the church, and remain in his confessional the whole morning, except while he was saying Mass. He invited young men to his room daily, and discoursed with them in the most winning way on the things of God. Then he would take them with him to some church, for Compline or Matins; and thus many youths and even men passed almost the whole day in his company. When some remonstrated with him for making himself so common, and for the noise he allowed his younger children to make, he said that he wished to have neither hour nor time to himself, and that if he could only keep his children from sin, they might chop wood on his back. And thus S. Philip's room was ever open to all who sought guidance, instruction, or refreshment, and men came to call it the school of holiness, and the sheltered home of Christian mirth. In this simple way, he drew souls to him as a magnet draws iron, and taught them how to sanctify themselves in the world and by means of their daily duties.

### Love of Souls.

The secret of S. Philip's power was that he loved the souls for whom Jesus died, and therefore sought their salvation with unsparing sacrifice of self, with tender sympathy, and a gentleness and patience never exhausted.

'My most spiritual penitents have been won to God by laying myself out, even at nights, for their conversion; and be assured that nothing brings such consolation and sweetness to souls that love God, as leaving Christ for Christ.' S. Philip.

Philip knew by revelation that a young nobleman, who was not one of his penitents, had been in the habit of making bad confessions. One day, when they were alone together, the Saint fixed his eyes upon him and began to weep. The youth's heart was softened and his conscience aroused, and he too burst into tears. For some time neither could speak; and then Philip, sobbing as he spoke, entreated him to enter into himself and make a good confession. The youth was touched with compunction, begged Philip to pray for him, and went at once and made a good confession. When he came back Philip said, ' Now, my son, you are quite changed; you have got back your good looks again; an expression he often used of those who had recovered the grace of God.

' The charity of Christ presseth us.'-2 Cor. V. 14.

## May 23 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** Reverence for the Exercises.

### Life

THE reports of the Jesuit missionaries inspired Philip with a wish to go out himself to India, labour there for souls, and if need be shed his blood for Christ; but a holy monk, whom he consulted, told him that it was the will of God that he should find his Indies in Rome. The Saint at once accepted his mission, and for sixty years never left Rome again. He toiled on simply, earnestly, joyously, amidst insult and opposition, gaining innumerable souls to God. As his own room became too small for the throng of his disciples he transferred their meetings to an oratory in the church of San Girolamo, and gave their exercises a set form. There was prayer mental and vocal, spiritual reading, instruction simple, solid, and varied, interspersed with bright and cheerful singing. Three times a week they took the discipline, in memory of our Lord's Passion; and thus, says Baronius, the Saint brought back in all their simplicity the days of the Primitive Church. Rich and poor, the wise and the simple, merchants and artisans, learned of him how to sanctify common life and to live in the world in union with God. Around his confessional, hanging on his lips, were the children of those who had been the turbulent oppressors of the Church. Jews, apostates, and heretics were brought into the one fold, and many a seemingly obdurate sinner owed his conversion to a chance visit to Philip's simple services at San Girolamo della Carità.

### Reverence for the Exercises.

' Our rules,' says F. Consolini, ' are the heart, soul, and spirit of the holy Father; and we should observe them as if we were obeying him.'

'Above all things, observe the rules which I have left you, and have a cordial love one for the other.' From S. Philip's will.

Besides the exercises at San Girolamo Philip trained his sons in works of charity. On Sundays and feasts, after Confession and Communion, he sent them off in bands to the different hospitals in Rome. On feast-days in the summer he would lead them to S. Onofrio, a hill commanding the city, and there the exercises were held, with music and innocent recreations. During the Carnival, and at other times of dissipation, he led them in procession to the seven great churches of Rome, chanting psalms and litanies as they went; and great multitudes, including princes and prelates of the Church, would join in, thus making reparation for the sins and follies of the people. And every Sunday one brother was sent to each of the seven churches, to pray for the intentions of the brotherhood.

'And I, brethren, when I came to you, came not in loftiness of speech or of wisdom, but in the showing of the Spirit and power.'-1 Cor. ii. 1, 5.

## May 24 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** Patient Trust in God.

### Life

IN 1564, at the request of the Florentines and by order of Pius IV., Philip took charge of their church of S. John, and sent thither Baronius and others of his sons, he himself remaining at S. Girolamo. Ten years later, the Florentines having built a spacious oratory at S. John's, prevailed on Philip to transfer the exercises to it, and they were more frequented and more fruitful than ever. But a wretched calumny, which had led the Florentines to think of taking their church from him, showed the Saint that the community now needed a secure foundation and a church of its own. Gregory XIII. gave him the church of S. Maria in Vallicella, and by a Bull dated July 15th, 1575, erected in it a congregation of secular priests, under the name of the Congregation of the Oratory. The church was small and ruinous, and Philip's first care was to rebuild it. He had no funds, but he had great faith. 'I know God will help me, ' he said. He refused to beg, but donations, unexpected and sufficient, came in as they were needed. The enemies of the Saint opposed him in every way, and even assaulted the workmen. Our Lady averted a terrible accident. Philip was miraculously cured of a dangerous illness by receiving Holy Communion; and on February 23d, 1577, within eighteen months from the laying of the foundation, Mass was sung in the grand church, still called the Chiesa Nuova.

### Patient Trust in God.

Throw yourself into God's hands,' said S. Philip, ' and be sure that if He wants anything of you, He will give you all that is necessary for His purpose.'

In this life there is no purgatory; it is either all hell or all paradise: for he who suffers tribulation with patience enjoys paradise; and he who does not, suffers hell.-S. Philip.

Adversity, said the Saint, is the surest mark of God's love; and each work of his life was in its turn signed with the Cross. At S. Girolamo the sacristans, two apostate monks, feeling his holiness an intolerable reproach to them, insulted him daily. They would hide his chalice, give him torn vestments, drive him from altar to altar, and push him rudely. Both were in the end converted by Philip's invincible patience. The visits to the seven churches were ridiculed and misrepresented; the sermons in the Oratory denounced as imprudent and unsound; and Philip was said to be an ambitious intriguing hypocrite. The exercises were once stopped, Philip was forbidden to hear confessions, and even threatened with imprisonment. But he uttered no word of complaint, sought neither help nor redress but from God, and every persecution advanced his work.

' The God of heaven He helpeth us, and we are His servants: let us rise up and build.'2 Esdras ii. 20.

## May 25 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** Submission of the Understanding.

### Life

So much did Philip shrink from being looked on as the founder of the Oratory that, for six long years after it was set up, he persisted in living away from it at S. Girolamo. At length, to the great joy of his sons, the Pope commanded him to take up his abode with them at the Vallicella on S. Cecilia's-day, 1583. He could not avoid being chosen Superior for life; but he stripped the office of all outward distinction, and would have no other title than that of ' Father." He directed that his sons should live as secular priests, without vows, bound together by the simple tie of charity; and that their aim should be the conversion and sanctification of souls by means of prayer, daily preaching, and frequentation of the Sacraments. He made few rules, but he would have those few perfectly kept. He had great horror of disobedience, and would keep no subject, however gifted in other respects, who did not at once gladly obey. He said, touching his forehead as he spoke, that a man's holiness lies really within the space of three fingers, meaning in the mortification of the understanding. Ile was always breaking down the pride of his children, priests or laymen, by penances or humiliations, often small in themselves, but always severe and searching as tests. It was noted that these were adapted with exquisite discrimination to the characters and needs of his penitents, and never failed of their effect.

### Submission of the Understanding.

S. Philip advised his children to be most careful in choosing a confessor, and then to obey him implicitly, discovering to him all their affections with freedom, sincerity, and simplicity, taking no resolution without his counsel.

'Obedience is a short road to perfection.'-S. Philip.

While Baronius was writing his 'Annals, the Pope forced on him a small annual sum towards the expenses of copying manuscripts. Philip at once seized on this to give him a lesson in obedience. He sent F. Bozio to tell him that he must now begin to contribute towards the expenses of the house. Baronius felt this keenly; he said the money was not his own, it was given for a special purpose; and that, rather than divert it to any other, he would leave the Congregation. ' Tell him,' said the Saint, ' that he must contribute or go; God has no need of men.' By F. Bozio's advice Baronius went to Philip, knelt before him, asked pardon for his hesitation, and offered him himself, all that he had and all that he might ever have. That is enough,' said the Saint; ' now go; I do not want your money; only learn another time to obey at once.' 'Have confidence in the Lord with all thy heart; and lean not on thy own prudence.' Prov. iii. 5.

## May 26 — S. Philip.

**Virtue:** The Grace of a good Death.

### Life

On May 15th, 1595, Philip said, ' In ten days I shall go.' He was in his eightieth year, apparently in perfect health, and his doctor said he had not seen him so well for ten years. But his work was done. Rome was entirely changed; the great religious Orders were filled with his penitents, Saints formed by him were winning souls to God, his congregation was established, and new houses were springing up; his silent toil of sixty years was bearing its abundant fruit. May 25th was the Feast of Corpus Christi. Philip heard confessions at his usual early hour, and took leave of his penitents with even more than his wonted tenderness, asking them to say a Rosary, and to pray for him when he was gone. As he began his last Mass, his gaze seemed fixed on some distant vision; at the Gloria he burst out into singing, and throughout the Mass he was as though he were chanting in the fulness of his joy. The day passed as usual. He said Matins of the morrow, and at eleven he went to bed, saying, ' Well, at last one must die.' At two o'clock F. Gallonio heard him move, and went to him. ' Antonio,' said the Saint, 'I am going now.' Baronius commended his soul to God, and then said, ' Father, you are going; have you nothing to say to us? Give us at least your b'essing.' Philip raised his hand, looked a while towards heaven; then turned his eyes around on his sons, as if giving his benediction, and closed them in death.

### The Grace of a good Death.

' O my father!' exclaimed a dying son of S. Philip, ' why did I not know thee long since? Why so late, O my father? We shall none of us know what S. Philip is to us till we come to die.

'Words cannot express the beauty of a soul which dies in the grace of God.'-S. Philip.

On the evening of Philip's canonization, March 12th, 1622, while a Capuchin was praying before his shrine, the Saint appeared to him all bright and glorious, with a company of holy souls about him. Emboldened by the gracious sight, the religious asked the Saint who his blessed companions were. Philip answered with a smile that they were fathers of his Congregation and brothers of the little Oratory, five of whom he was that day escorting from purgatory; and he bade him tell his sons to observe the holy institute in all things, for that it was most pleasing to the Divine Majesty; and that they should know for their comfort that up to that day, by the grace of God, not one of his Congregation had been lost.

'Whether we live, we live to the Lord; or whether we die, we die to the Lord.'-Rom. xiv. 8.

## Prayers to S. Philip

Prayers to S. Philip. O holy Father S. Philip, I beseech thee, by the abundance of the gifts thou didst receive from God, both of nature and grace, and by the glory thou dost enjoy in heaven, to help me to obtain the joys of paradise. Pater. Ave. Gloria. O most loving Father S. Philip, I beseech thee, by the gladness which fills thy heart in enjoying the presence of the Most Holy Virgin in heaven, to obtain for me a true devotion to her, that she may look upon me as her servant and son. Pater. Ave. Gloria. O most glorious Father S. Philip, I beseech thee, by the burning zeal thou hadst for the salvation of souls, to obtain for me the grace to love God truly from my heart, and ever to weep for my sins even to my life's end, that I may at last receive the crown He has prepared for those who love Him. Pater. Ave. Gloria. V. S. Philip, pray for us. R. That we may be made worthy of the promises of Christ. LET US PRAY. O Lord, we beseech Thee, let the Holy Spirit inflame us with the same fire with which He did wonderfully penetrate the heart of Blessed Philip, Thy Confessor; through Christ our Lord. Amen.
